<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>
<p>Contemporaries referred to the empire founded by <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid</a> empire,<sup id="cite_ref-MSA_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSA-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> which reflected the heritage of his dynasty, and this was the term preferred by the Mughals themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Avari_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Avari-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mughal designation for their own dynasty was <b>Gurkani</b> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl">گورکانیان</span>&#8206;, <i>Gūrkāniyān</i>, meaning "sons-in-law").<sup id="cite_ref-Thackston_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thackston-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The use of "Mughal" derived from the Arabic and Persian corruption of "<a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongol</a>", and it emphasised the Mongol origins of the Timurid dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-Hodgson_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgson-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The term gained currency during the 19th century, but remains disputed by <a href="/wiki/Indologists" class="mw-redirect" title="Indologists">Indologists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RA_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RA-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Similar terms had been used to refer to the empire, including "Mogul" and "Moghul".<sup id="cite_ref-persianatemogul_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-persianatemogul-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutherford2010_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutherford2010-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Babur's ancestors were sharply distinguished from the classical Mongols insofar as they were oriented towards <a href="/wiki/Persian_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian culture">Persian</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Turco-Mongol" class="mw-redirect" title="Turco-Mongol">Turco-Mongol</a> culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Canfield_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canfield-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another name for the empire was <a href="/wiki/Hindustan" title="Hindustan">Hindustan</a>, which was documented in the <a href="/wiki/Ain-i-Akbari" title="Ain-i-Akbari">Ain-i-Akbari</a>, and which has been described as the closest to an official name for the empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Vanina_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vanina-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In the west, the term "<a href="/wiki/Mughal_emperors" title="Mughal emperors">Mughal</a>" was used for the emperor, and by extension, the empire as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-Fontana_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fontana-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mughal_emperors" title="Mughal emperors">Mughal emperors</a></div>
<h3><span id="Babur_and_Humayun_.281526.E2.80.931556.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Babur_and_Humayun_(1526–1556)">Babur and Humayun (1526–1556)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zahir_al-Din_Muhammad_Babur_-_Babur_and_His_Warriors_Visiting_a_Hindu_Temple_-_Walters_W59622B_-_Full_Page.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Zahir_al-Din_Muhammad_Babur_-_Babur_and_His_Warriors_Visiting_a_Hindu_Temple_-_Walters_W59622B_-_Full_Page.jpg/250px-Zahir_al-Din_Muhammad_Babur_-_Babur_and_His_Warriors_Visiting_a_Hindu_Temple_-_Walters_W59622B_-_Full_Page.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="379" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Zahir_al-Din_Muhammad_Babur_-_Babur_and_His_Warriors_Visiting_a_Hindu_Temple_-_Walters_W59622B_-_Full_Page.jpg/375px-Zahir_al-Din_Muhammad_Babur_-_Babur_and_His_Warriors_Visiting_a_Hindu_Temple_-_Walters_W59622B_-_Full_Page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Zahir_al-Din_Muhammad_Babur_-_Babur_and_His_Warriors_Visiting_a_Hindu_Temple_-_Walters_W59622B_-_Full_Page.jpg/500px-Zahir_al-Din_Muhammad_Babur_-_Babur_and_His_Warriors_Visiting_a_Hindu_Temple_-_Walters_W59622B_-_Full_Page.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1186" data-file-height="1799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zahir_al-Din_Muhammad_Babur_-_Babur_and_His_Warriors_Visiting_a_Hindu_Temple_-_Walters_W59622B_-_Full_Page.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>, the founder of the Mughal Empire, and his warriors visiting a <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple" title="Hindu temple">Hindu temple</a> in the Indian subcontinent.</div></div></div>
<p>The Mughal Empire was founded by <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> (reigned 1526–1530), a Central Asian ruler who was descended from the Turco-Mongol conqueror <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> (the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a>) on his father's side, and from <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> on his mother's side.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Ousted from his ancestral domains in Central Asia, Babur turned to India to satisfy his ambitions.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
He established himself in <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> and then pushed steadily southward into India from <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pass" title="Khyber Pass">Khyber Pass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Babur's forces occupied much of northern India after his victory at <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Panipat" title="First Battle of Panipat">Panipat</a> in 1526.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The preoccupation with wars and military campaigns, however, did not allow the new emperor to consolidate the gains he had made in India.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The instability of the empire became evident under his son, <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a> (reigned 1530–1556), who was forced into exile in Persia by rebels. The <a href="/wiki/Sur_Empire" title="Sur Empire">Sur Empire</a> (1540–1555), founded by <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a> (reigned 1540–1545), briefly interrupted Mughal rule.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Humayun's exile in Persia established diplomatic ties between the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavid Dynasty">Safavid</a> and Mughal Courts, and led to increasing Persian cultural influence in the Mughal Empire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Humayun's triumphant return from Persia in 1555 restored Mughal rule, but he died in an accident the next year.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Akbar_to_Aurangzeb_.281556.E2.80.931707.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Akbar_to_Aurangzeb_(1556–1707)">Akbar to Aurangzeb (1556–1707)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> (reigned 1556–1605) was born Jalal-ud-din Muhammad<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> in the Rajput <a href="/wiki/Umarkot_Fort" title="Umarkot Fort">Umarkot Fort</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> to Humayun and his wife <a href="/wiki/Hamida_Banu_Begum" title="Hamida Banu Begum">Hamida Banu Begum</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian people">Persian</a> princess.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Akbar succeeded to the throne under a regent, <a href="/wiki/Bairam_Khan" title="Bairam Khan">Bairam Khan</a>, who helped consolidate the Mughal Empire in India. Through warfare and diplomacy, Akbar was able to extend the empire in all directions and controlled almost the entire Indian subcontinent north of the <a href="/wiki/Godavari_River" title="Godavari River">Godavari River</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> He created a new ruling elite loyal to him, implemented a modern administration, and encouraged cultural developments. He increased trade with European trading companies.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> India developed a strong and stable economy, leading to commercial expansion and economic development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Akbar allowed freedom of religion at his court, and attempted to resolve socio-political and cultural differences in his empire by establishing a new religion, <a href="/wiki/Din-i-Ilahi" class="mw-redirect" title="Din-i-Ilahi">Din-i-Ilahi</a>, with strong characteristics of a ruler cult.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> He left his son an internally stable state, which was in the midst of its golden age, but before long signs of political weakness would emerge.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a> (born Salim,<sup id="cite_ref-Mohammada_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mohammada-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> reigned 1605–1627) was born to Akbar and his wife <a href="/wiki/Mariam-uz-Zamani" title="Mariam-uz-Zamani">Mariam-uz-Zamani</a>, an Indian <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> princess.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> He "was addicted to opium, neglected the affairs of the state, and came under the influence of rival court cliques".<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> (reigned 1628–1658) was born to Jahangir and his wife <a href="/wiki/Jagat_Gosaini" class="mw-redirect" title="Jagat Gosaini">Jagat Gosaini</a>, a Rajput princess.<sup id="cite_ref-Mohammada_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mohammada-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> During the reign of Shah Jahan, the splendour of the Mughal court reached its peak, as exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>. The cost of maintaining the court, however, began to exceed the revenue coming in.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/250px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="453" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/375px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg/500px-Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2275" data-file-height="4123" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jesuits_at_Akbar%27s_court.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> holds a religious assembly of different faiths in the <a href="/wiki/Ibadat_Khana" title="Ibadat Khana">Ibadat Khana</a> in Fatehpur Sikri.</div></div></div>
<p>Shah Jahan's eldest son, the liberal <a href="/wiki/Dara_Shikoh" title="Dara Shikoh">Dara Shikoh</a>, became regent in 1658, as a result of his father's illness.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Dara championed a syncretistic Hindu-Muslim culture. With the support of the Islamic orthodoxy, however, a younger son of Shah Jahan, <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> (reigned 1658–1707), seized the throne. Aurangzeb defeated Dara in 1659 and had him executed.<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Although Shah Jahan fully recovered from his illness, Aurangzeb declared him incompetent to rule and had him imprisoned. During Aurangzeb's reign, the empire gained political strength once more and became the world's most powerful economy. Aurangzeb fully established <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a> by compiling the <a href="/wiki/Fatwa_Alamgiri" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatwa Alamgiri">Fatwa Alamgiri</a>. He expanded the empire to include almost the whole of South Asia,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> but at his death in 1707, "many parts of the empire were in open revolt".<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Aurangzeb is considered India's most controversial king,<sup id="cite_ref-Truschke_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Truschke-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2019)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> with some historians<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="Who says this? (July 2019)">weasel&#160;words</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> arguing his religious conservatism and intolerance undermined the stability of Mughal society,<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> while other historians question this, noting that he built <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple" title="Hindu temple">Hindu temples</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Copland2013_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copland2013-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> employed significantly more <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindus</a> in his imperial bureaucracy than his predecessors did, opposed bigotry against Hindus and <a href="/wiki/Shia_Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia Muslims">Shia Muslims</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Truschke_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Truschke-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>50</span></sup> and married Hindu Rajput princess <a href="/wiki/Nawab_Bai" title="Nawab Bai">Nawab Bai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mohammada_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mohammada-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Decline_.281707.E2.80.931857.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_(1707–1857)"><span id="Decline">Decline (1707–1857)</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_Zafar" title="Bahadur Shah Zafar">Bahadur Shah Zafar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Shah_Jahan_and_Prince_Alamgir_(Aurangzeb)_in_Mughal_Court,_1650.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Emperor_Shah_Jahan_and_Prince_Alamgir_%28Aurangzeb%29_in_Mughal_Court%2C_1650.jpg/220px-Emperor_Shah_Jahan_and_Prince_Alamgir_%28Aurangzeb%29_in_Mughal_Court%2C_1650.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="327" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Emperor_Shah_Jahan_and_Prince_Alamgir_%28Aurangzeb%29_in_Mughal_Court%2C_1650.jpg/330px-Emperor_Shah_Jahan_and_Prince_Alamgir_%28Aurangzeb%29_in_Mughal_Court%2C_1650.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Emperor_Shah_Jahan_and_Prince_Alamgir_%28Aurangzeb%29_in_Mughal_Court%2C_1650.jpg/440px-Emperor_Shah_Jahan_and_Prince_Alamgir_%28Aurangzeb%29_in_Mughal_Court%2C_1650.jpg 2x" data-file-width="516" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Shah_Jahan_and_Prince_Alamgir_(Aurangzeb)_in_Mughal_Court,_1650.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> and Prince <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> in Mughal Court, 1650</div></div></div>
<p>Aurangzeb's son, <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_I" title="Bahadur Shah I">Bahadur Shah I</a>, repealed the religious policies of his father, and attempted to reform the administration. "However, after his death in 1712, the Mughal dynasty sank into chaos and violent feuds. In 1719 alone, four emperors successively ascended the throne".<sup id="cite_ref-Berndl_42-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berndl-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Shah" title="Muhammad Shah">Muhammad Shah</a> (reigned 1719–1748), the empire began to break up, and vast tracts of central India passed from Mughal to <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">Maratha</a> hands. The far-off <a href="/wiki/Nadir_Shah%27s_invasion_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Nadir Shah&#39;s invasion of India">Indian campaign</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nadir_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Nadir Shah">Nadir Shah</a>, who had priorly reestablished Iranian <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> over most of West Asia, the Caucasus, and Central Asia, culminated with the <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Delhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sack of Delhi">Sack of Delhi</a> and shattered the remnants of Mughal power and prestige. Many of the empire's elites now sought to control their own affairs, and broke away to form independent kingdoms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> But, according to <a href="/wiki/Sugata_Bose" title="Sugata Bose">Sugata Bose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayesha_Jalal" title="Ayesha Jalal">Ayesha Jalal</a>, the Mughal Emperor continued to be the highest manifestation of sovereignty. Not only the Muslim gentry, but the Maratha, Hindu, and Sikh leaders took part in ceremonial acknowledgements of the emperor as the sovereign of India.<sup id="cite_ref-MSA2_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSA2-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, some regional polities within the increasingly fragmented Mughal Empire, involved themselves and the state within global conflicts, leading only to defeat and loss of territory during the <a href="/wiki/Carnatic_Wars" title="Carnatic Wars">Carnatic Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_War" title="Bengal War">Bengal War</a>.
</p><p>The Mughal Emperor <a href="/wiki/Shah_Alam_II" title="Shah Alam II">Shah Alam II</a> (1759–1806) made futile attempts to reverse the Mughal decline but ultimately had to seek the protection of the Emir of Afghanistan, Ahmed Shah Abdali, which led to the <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Panipat" title="Third Battle of Panipat">Third Battle of Panipat</a> between the Maratha Empire and the Afghans led by Abdali in 1761. In 1771, the Marathas recaptured Delhi from Afghan control and in 1784 they officially became the protectors of the emperor in Delhi,<sup id="cite_ref-Rathod1994_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rathod1994-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> a state of affairs that continued further until after the <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Maratha_War" title="Third Anglo-Maratha War">Third Anglo-Maratha War</a>. Thereafter, the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> became the protectors of the Mughal dynasty in Delhi.<sup id="cite_ref-MSA2_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSA2-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The British East India <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_India" title="Company rule in India">Company</a> took control of the former Mughal province of Bengal-Bihar in 1793 after it abolished local rule (Nizamat) that lasted until 1858, marking the beginning of British colonial era over the Indian Subcontinent. By 1857 a considerable part of former Mughal India was under the East India Company's control. After a crushing defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">war of 1857–1858</a> which he nominally led, the last Mughal, <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_Zafar" title="Bahadur Shah Zafar">Bahadur Shah Zafar</a>, was deposed by the British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> and exiled in 1858. Through the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India_Act_1858" title="Government of India Act 1858">Government of India Act 1858</a> the <a href="/wiki/British_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="British Crown">British Crown</a> assumed direct control of East India Company-held territories in India in the form of the new <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>. In 1876 the British <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> assumed the title of <a href="/wiki/Empress_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Empress of India">Empress of India</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_decline">Causes of decline</span></h3>
<p>Historians have offered numerous explanations for the rapid collapse of the Mughal Empire between 1707 and 1720, after a century of growth and prosperity. In fiscal terms the throne lost the revenues needed to pay its chief officers, the emirs (nobles) and their entourages. The emperor lost authority, as the widely scattered imperial officers lost confidence in the central authorities, and made their own deals with local men of influence. The imperial army, bogged down in long, futile wars against the more aggressive <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">Marathas</a>, lost its fighting spirit. Finally came a series of violent political feuds over control of the throne. After the execution of emperor Farrukhsiyar in 1719, local Mughal successor states took power in region after region.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary chroniclers bewailed the decay they witnessed, a theme picked up by the first British historians who wanted to underscore the need for a British-led rejuvenation.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_views_on_the_decline">Modern views on the decline</span></h4>
<p>Since the 1970s historians have taken multiple approaches to the decline, with little consensus on which factor was dominant. The psychological interpretations emphasise depravity in high places, excessive luxury, and increasingly narrow views that left the rulers unprepared for an external challenge. A Marxist school (led by <a href="/wiki/Irfan_Habib" title="Irfan Habib">Irfan Habib</a> and based at <a href="/wiki/Aligarh_Muslim_University" title="Aligarh Muslim University">Aligarh Muslim University</a>) emphasises excessive exploitation of the peasantry by the rich, which stripped away the will and the means to support the regime.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Karen Leonard has focused on the failure of the regime to work with Hindu bankers, whose financial support was increasingly needed; the bankers then helped the Maratha and the British.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In a religious interpretation, some scholars argue that the Hindu powers revolted against the rule of a Muslim dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Finally, other scholars argue that the very prosperity of the Empire inspired the provinces to achieve a high degree of independence, thus weakening the imperial court.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_G._Williamson" title="Jeffrey G. Williamson">Jeffrey G. Williamson</a> has argued that the <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_India" title="Economic history of India">Indian economy</a> went through <a href="/wiki/Deindustrialization" title="Deindustrialization">deindustrialization</a> in the latter half of the 18th century as an indirect outcome of the collapse of the Mughal Empire, with <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British rule</a> later causing further deindustrialization.<sup id="cite_ref-williamson_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williamson-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> According to Williamson, the decline of the Mughal Empire led to a decline in agricultural productivity, which drove up food prices, then <a href="/wiki/Real_versus_nominal_value_(economics)" title="Real versus nominal value (economics)">nominal</a> wages, and then textile prices, which led to India losing a share of the world textile market to Britain even before it had superior <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">factory technology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_G._Williamson_2011_91_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey_G._Williamson_2011_91-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Indian textiles, however, still maintained a competitive advantage over British textiles up until the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subah" title="Subah">Subah</a></div>
<p><i>Subah</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Urdu</a>: صوبہ) was the term for a province in the Mughal Empire. The word is derived from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>. The governor of a <i>Subah</i> was known as a <i><a href="/wiki/Subahdar" title="Subahdar">subahdar</a></i> (sometimes also referred to as a "<i>Subah</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-Whitworth1885_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitworth1885-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>), which later became <i><a href="/wiki/Subedar" title="Subedar">subedar</a></i> to refer to an officer in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Army" title="Indian Army">Indian Army</a>. The <i>subahs</i> were established by <a href="/wiki/Padshah" class="mw-redirect" title="Padshah">padshah</a> (emperor) <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> during his administrative reforms of 1572–1580; initially they numbered 12, but his conquests expanded the number of <i>subahs</i> to 15 by the end of his reign. <i>Subahs</i> were divided into <i><a href="/wiki/Sarkar_(country_subdivision)" title="Sarkar (country subdivision)">Sarkars</a></i>, or districts. <i>Sarkars</i> were further divided into <i><a href="/wiki/Pargana" title="Pargana">Parganas</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Mahalla" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahalla">Mahals</a></i>. His successors, most notably <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a>, expanded the number of <i>subahs</i> further through their conquests. As the empire began to dissolve in the early 18th century, many <i>subahs</i> became effectively independent, or were conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Maratha" class="mw-redirect" title="Maratha">Marathas</a> or the <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British</a>.
</p><p>The original twelve subahs created as a result of administrative reform by Akbar:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Agra_Subah" title="Agra Subah">Agra Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ajmer_subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ajmer subah">Ajmer subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Awadh_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Awadh Subah">Awadh Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bihar_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Bihar Subah">Bihar Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Delhi_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Delhi Subah">Delhi Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gujarat_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Gujarat Subah">Gujarat Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kabul_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Kabul Subah">Kabul Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Illahabad_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Illahabad Subah">Illahabad Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lahore_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Lahore Subah">Lahore Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Malwa_Subah" title="Malwa Subah">Malwa Subah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Multan_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Multan Subah">Multan Subah</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_India" title="Economic history of India">Economic history of India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_economy_of_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Timeline of the economy of the Indian subcontinent">Timeline of the economy of the Indian subcontinent</a></div>
<p>The Indian economy was large and prosperous under the Mughal Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> During the Mughal era, the <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) of India in 1600 was estimated at about 22% of the <a href="/wiki/World_economy" title="World economy">world economy</a>, the second largest in the world, behind only <a href="/wiki/Ming_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming China">Ming China</a> but larger than Europe. By 1700, the GDP of Mughal India had risen to 24% of the world economy, the largest in the world, larger than both <a href="/wiki/Qing_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Qing China">Qing China</a> and Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddison2003_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddison2003-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Mughal India was the world leader in manufacturing,<sup id="cite_ref-Parthasarathi_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parthasarathi-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> producing about 25% of the world's industrial output up until the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Williamson_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williamson-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> India's GDP growth increased under the Mughal Empire, with India's GDP having a faster growth rate during the Mughal era than in the 1,500 years prior to the Mughal era.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddison2003_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddison2003-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Mughal India's economy has been described as a form of <a href="/wiki/Proto-industrialization" title="Proto-industrialization">proto-industrialization</a>, like that of 18th-century Western Europe prior to the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-voss_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-voss-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mughals were responsible for building an extensive road system, creating a uniform currency, and the unification of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>185–204</span></sup> The empire had an extensive road network, which was vital to the economic infrastructure, built by a <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">public works</a> department set up by the Mughals which designed, constructed and maintained roads linking towns and cities across the empire, making trade easier to conduct.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The main base of the empire's collective wealth was agricultural taxes, instituted by the third Mughal emperor, Akbar.<sup id="cite_ref-Stein2010-3_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-3-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-1_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-1-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  These taxes, which amounted to well over half the output of a peasant cultivator,<sup id="cite_ref-Stein2010-4_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2010-4-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> were paid in the well-regulated silver currency,<sup id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-2_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-2-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> and caused peasants and artisans to enter larger markets.<sup id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006-3_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-3-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coinage">Coinage</span></h3>
<p>The Mughals adopted and standardized the <a href="/wiki/Rupee" title="Rupee">rupee</a> (<i>rupiya</i>, or silver) and <a href="/wiki/Dam_(Indian_coin)" title="Dam (Indian coin)">dam</a> (copper) currencies introduced by <a href="/wiki/Sur_Empire" title="Sur Empire">Sur</a> Emperor <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a> during his brief rule.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The currency was initially 48 dams to a single rupee in the beginning of Akbar's reign, before it later became 38 dams to a rupee in the 1580s, with the dam's value rising further in the 17th century as a result of new industrial uses for copper, such as in bronze cannons and brass utensils. The dam was initially the most common coin in Akbar's time, before being replaced by the rupee as the most common coin in succeeding reigns.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The dam's value was later worth 30 to a rupee towards the end of Jahangir's reign, and then 16 to a rupee by the 1660s.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The Mughals minted coins with high purity, never dropping below 96%, and without <a href="/wiki/Debasement" title="Debasement">debasement</a> until the 1720s.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards2003_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2003-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite India having its own stocks of gold and silver, the Mughals produced minimal gold of their own, but mostly minted coins from imported <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a>, as a result of the empire's strong export-driven economy, with global demand for Indian agricultural and industrial products drawing a steady stream of <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metals</a> into India.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Around 80% of Mughal India's imports were bullion, mostly silver,<sup id="cite_ref-Tracy1997_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tracy1997-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> with major sources of imported bullion including the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> and Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-Richards2003_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2003-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> which in turn imported large quantities of textiles and silk from the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a> province.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour">Labour</span></h3>
<p>The Mughal Empire's <a href="/wiki/Workforce" title="Workforce">workforce</a> in the early 17th century consisted of about 64% in the <a href="/wiki/Primary_sector" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary sector">primary sector</a> (including agriculture), over 11% in the <a href="/wiki/Secondary_sector" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary sector">secondary sector</a> (manufacturing), and about 25% in the <a href="/wiki/Tertiary_sector_of_the_economy" title="Tertiary sector of the economy">tertiary sector</a> (service).<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Mughal India's workforce had a higher percentage in the non-primary sector than Europe's workforce did at the time; agriculture accounted for 65–90% of Europe's workforce in 1700, and 65–75% in 1750, including 65% of England's workforce in 1750.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Historian Shireen Moosvi estimates that in terms of contributions to the Mughal economy, in the late 16th century, the primary sector contributed 52%, the secondary sector 18% and the tertiary sector 29%; the secondary sector contributed a higher percentage than in early 20th-century <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a>, where the secondary sector only contributed 11% to the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015433_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015433-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In terms of urban-rural divide, 18% of Mughal India's labour force were urban and 82% were rural, contributing 52% and 48% to the economy, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Real_wages" title="Real wages">Real wages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Living_standards" class="mw-redirect" title="Living standards">living standards</a> in 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal Bengal">Mughal Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a> were higher than in Britain, which in turn had the highest living standards in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Parthasarathi38_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parthasarathi38-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-williamson_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williamson-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> According to economic historian <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bairoch" title="Paul Bairoch">Paul Bairoch</a>, India as well as China had a higher <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross national product">GNP</a> per capita than Europe up until the late 18th century,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> before Western European per-capita income pulled ahead after 1800.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> According to Moosvi, Mughal India also had a per-capita income 1.24% higher in the late 16th century than British India did in the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015432_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoosvi2015432-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> However, in a system where wealth was hoarded by elites, wages were depressed for <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">manual labour</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-moosvi_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moosvi-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> though no less than labour wages in Europe at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Parthasarathi38_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parthasarathi38-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In Mughal India, there was a generally tolerant attitude towards manual labourers, with some religious cults in northern India proudly asserting a high status for manual labour. While <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> also existed, it was limited largely to household servants.<sup id="cite_ref-moosvi_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moosvi-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<p>Indian agricultural production increased under the Mughal Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> A variety of crops were grown, including food crops such as wheat, rice, and <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, and non-food <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crops</a> such as cotton, <a href="/wiki/Indigofera_tinctoria" title="Indigofera tinctoria">indigo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>. By the mid-17th century, Indian cultivators begun to extensively grow two new crops from the Americas, maize and tobacco.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mughal administration emphasized <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_reform" title="Agrarian reform">agrarian reform</a>, which began under the non-Mughal emperor Sher Shah Suri, the work of which Akbar adopted and furthered with more reforms. The civil administration was organized in a hierarchical manner on the basis of merit, with promotions based on performance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagazaArgyriades2009129_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagazaArgyriades2009129-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The Mughal government funded the building of <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> systems across the empire, which produced much higher <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yields</a> and increased the net revenue base, leading to increased agricultural production.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A major Mughal reform introduced by Akbar was a new land revenue system called <i>zabt</i>. He replaced the <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tribute</a> system, previously common in India and used by <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokugawa Japan">Tokugawa Japan</a> at the time, with a monetary tax system based on a uniform currency.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards2003_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2003-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The revenue system was biased in favour of higher value cash crops such as cotton, indigo, <a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>, tree-crops, and opium, providing state incentives to grow cash crops, in addition to rising market demand.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Under the <i>zabt</i> system, the Mughals also conducted extensive <a href="/wiki/Cadastral_surveying" title="Cadastral surveying">cadastral surveying</a> to assess the area of land under <a href="/wiki/Plow" class="mw-redirect" title="Plow">plow</a> cultivation, with the Mughal state encouraging greater land cultivation by offering tax-free periods to those who brought new land under cultivation.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards2003_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2003-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The expansion of agriculture and cultivation continued under later Mughal emperors including Aurangzeb, whose 1665 <a href="/wiki/Firman" title="Firman">firman</a> edict stated: "the entire elevated attention and desires of the Emperor are devoted to the increase in the population and cultivation of the Empire and the welfare of the whole peasantry and the entire people."<sup id="cite_ref-Ludden96_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ludden96-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mughal agriculture was in some ways advanced compared to European agriculture at the time, exemplified by the common use of the <a href="/wiki/Seed_drill" title="Seed drill">seed drill</a> among Indian peasants before its adoption in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> While the average peasant across the world was only skilled in growing very few crops, the average Indian peasant was skilled in growing a wide variety of food and non-food crops, increasing their productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Indian peasants were also quick to adapt to profitable new crops, such as maize and tobacco from the New World being rapidly adopted and widely cultivated across Mughal India between 1600 and 1650. <a href="/wiki/Bengali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali people">Bengali</a> farmers rapidly learned techniques of <a href="/wiki/Mulberry" class="mw-redirect" title="Mulberry">mulberry</a> cultivation and <a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">sericulture</a>, establishing <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a> as a major silk-producing region of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sugar_mill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar mill">Sugar mills</a> appeared in India shortly before the Mughal era. Evidence for the use of a <a href="/wiki/Draw_bar" class="mw-redirect" title="Draw bar">draw bar</a> for sugar-milling appears at Delhi in 1540, but may also date back earlier, and was mainly used in the northern Indian subcontinent. Geared sugar <a href="/wiki/Roller_mill" title="Roller mill">rolling mills</a> first appeared in Mughal India, using the principle of rollers as well as <a href="/wiki/Worm_gear" class="mw-redirect" title="Worm gear">worm gearing</a>, by the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-K8kO4J3mXUAC_page_53_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K8kO4J3mXUAC_page_53-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to evidence cited by the economic historians <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Wallerstein" title="Immanuel Wallerstein">Immanuel Wallerstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irfan_Habib" title="Irfan Habib">Irfan Habib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percival_Spear" title="Percival Spear">Percival Spear</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ashok_Desai" title="Ashok Desai">Ashok Desai</a>, per-capita agricultural output and standards of consumption in 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Mughal_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal India">Mughal India</a> were higher than in 17th-century Europe and early 20th-century <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The increased agricultural productivity led to lower food prices. In turn, this benefited the Indian <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a>. Compared to Britain, the price of grain was about one-half in South India and one-third in Bengal, in terms of silver coinage. This resulted in lower silver coin prices for Indian textiles, giving them a price advantage in global markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Parthasarathi38_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parthasarathi38-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_manufacturing">Industrial manufacturing</span></h3>
<p>Up until the 18th century, Mughal India was the most important center of manufacturing in <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Parthasarathi_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parthasarathi-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Up until 1750, India produced about 25% of the world's industrial output.<sup id="cite_ref-williamson_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williamson-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manufactured_goods" class="mw-redirect" title="Manufactured goods">Manufactured goods</a> and cash crops from the Mughal Empire were sold throughout the world. Key industries included textiles, <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a>, and steel. Processed products included cotton textiles, <a href="/wiki/Yarn" title="Yarn">yarns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thread_(yarn)" title="Thread (yarn)">thread</a>, silk, <a href="/wiki/Jute" title="Jute">jute</a> products, <a href="/wiki/Metalware" class="mw-redirect" title="Metalware">metalware</a>, and foods such as sugar, oils and butter.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The growth of manufacturing industries in the Indian subcontinent during the Mughal era in the 17th–18th centuries has been referred to as a form of <a href="/wiki/Proto-industrialization" title="Proto-industrialization">proto-industrialization</a>, similar to 18th-century Western Europe prior to the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-voss_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-voss-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">early modern Europe</a>, there was significant demand for products from Mughal India, particularly cotton textiles, as well as goods such as spices, peppers, <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a>, silks, and <a href="/wiki/Saltpeter" class="mw-redirect" title="Saltpeter">saltpeter</a> (for use in <a href="/wiki/Munitions" class="mw-redirect" title="Munitions">munitions</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1650%E2%80%931700_in_Western_European_fashion" title="1650–1700 in Western European fashion">European fashion</a>, for example, became increasingly dependent on Mughal Indian textiles and silks. From the late 17th century to the early 18th century, Mughal India accounted for 95% of <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">British imports</a> from Asia, and the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a> province alone accounted for 40% of <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch imports</a> from Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, there was very little demand for European goods in Mughal India, which was largely self-sufficient, thus Europeans had very little to offer, except for some <a href="/wiki/Woolen" title="Woolen">woolens</a>, unprocessed metals and a few luxury items. The trade imbalance caused Europeans to export large quantities of gold and silver to Mughal India in order to pay for South Asian imports.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Indian goods, especially those from Bengal, were also exported in large quantities to other Asian markets, such as <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Textile_industry">Textile industry</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Muslin_trade_in_Bengal" title="Muslin trade in Bengal">Muslin trade in Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_clothing" title="Mughal clothing">Mughal clothing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muslin_men.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Muslin_men.jpg/220px-Muslin_men.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Muslin_men.jpg/330px-Muslin_men.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Muslin_men.jpg/440px-Muslin_men.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="621" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muslin_men.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mughal princes wearing <a href="/wiki/Muslin" title="Muslin">muslin</a> <a href="/wiki/Mughal_clothing" title="Mughal clothing">robes</a> in 1665 CE.</div></div></div>
<p>The largest manufacturing industry in the Mughal Empire was <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">textile manufacturing</a>, particularly cotton textile manufacturing, which included the production of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/piece_goods" class="extiw" title="wikt:piece goods">piece goods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calico" title="Calico">calicos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Muslin" title="Muslin">muslins</a>, available unbleached and in a variety of colours. The cotton <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a> was responsible for a large part of the empire's international trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> India had a 25% share of the global textile trade in the early 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Indian cotton textiles were the most important manufactured goods in world trade in the 18th century, consumed across the world from the Americas to Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Parthasarathi_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parthasarathi-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> By the early 18th century, Mughal Indian textiles were clothing people across the Indian subcontinent, Southeast Asia, Europe, the Americas, Africa, and the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_G._Williamson_2011_91_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey_G._Williamson_2011_91-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The most important center of cotton production was the Bengal province, particularly around its capital city of <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Eaton_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eaton-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bengal accounted for more than 50% of textiles and around 80% of silks imported by the Dutch from Asia,<sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Bengali silk and cotton textiles were exported in large quantities to Europe, Indonesia, and Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>202</span></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Muslin_trade_in_Bengal" title="Muslin trade in Bengal">Bengali muslin</a> textiles from Dhaka were sold in Central Asia, where they were known as "daka" textiles.<sup id="cite_ref-Eaton_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eaton-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Indian textiles dominated the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a> for centuries, were sold in the Atlantic Ocean trade, and had a 38% share of the West African trade in the early 18th century, while Indian calicos were a major force in Europe, and Indian textiles accounted for 20% of total English trade with Southern Europe in the early 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-williamson_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williamson-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Worm_gear" class="mw-redirect" title="Worm gear">worm gear</a> roller <a href="/wiki/Cotton_gin" title="Cotton gin">cotton gin</a>, which was invented in India during the early <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> era of the 13th–14th centuries, came into use in the Mughal Empire some time around the 16th century,<sup id="cite_ref-K8kO4J3mXUAC_page_53_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K8kO4J3mXUAC_page_53-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and is still used in India through to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-LakGin_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LakGin-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Another innovation, the incorporation of the <a href="/wiki/Crank_(mechanism)" title="Crank (mechanism)">crank</a> handle in the cotton gin, first appeared in India some time during the late Delhi Sultanate or the early Mughal Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The production of cotton, which may have largely been spun in the villages and then taken to towns in the form of yarn to be woven into cloth textiles, was advanced by the diffusion of the <a href="/wiki/Spinning_wheel" title="Spinning wheel">spinning wheel</a> across India shortly before the Mughal era, lowering the costs of yarn and helping to increase demand for cotton. The diffusion of the spinning wheel, and the incorporation of the worm gear and crank handle into the roller cotton gin, led to greatly expanded Indian cotton textile production during the Mughal era.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipbuilding_industry">Shipbuilding industry</span></h4>
<p>Mughal India had a large <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> industry, which was also largely centered in the Bengal province. Economic historian Indrajit Ray estimates shipbuilding output of Bengal during the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries at 223,250&#160;tons annually, compared with 23,061&#160;tons produced in nineteen colonies in North America from 1769 to 1771.<sup id="cite_ref-ray174_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ray174-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> He also assesses ship repairing as very advanced in Bengal.<sup id="cite_ref-ray174_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ray174-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Indian shipbuilding, particularly in Bengal, was advanced compared to European shipbuilding at the time, with Indians selling ships to European firms. An important innovation in shipbuilding was the introduction of a <a href="/wiki/Flush_deck" title="Flush deck">flushed deck</a> design in Bengal rice ships, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">hulls</a> that were stronger and less prone to leak than the structurally weak hulls of traditional European ships built with a stepped <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">deck</a> design. The British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> later duplicated the flushed deck and hull designs of Bengal rice ships in the 1760s, leading to significant improvements in <a href="/wiki/Seaworthiness" class="mw-redirect" title="Seaworthiness">seaworthiness</a> and navigation for European ships during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bengal_Subah">Bengal Subah</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Muslin_trade_in_Bengal" title="Muslin trade in Bengal">Muslin trade in Bengal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg/220px-Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg/330px-Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg/440px-Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boro_Katra_4_by_Ashif_Siddique.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Bara_Katra" title="Bara Katra">Great Caravanserai</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a> province was especially prosperous from the time of its takeover by the Mughals in 1590 until the British East India Company seized control in 1757.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> It was the Mughal Empire's wealthiest province,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> and the economic powerhouse of the Mughal Empire, estimated to have generated up to 50% of the empire's GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Domestically, much of India depended on Bengali products such as rice, silks and cotton textiles. Overseas, Europeans depended on Bengali products such as cotton textiles, silks and opium; Bengal accounted for 40% of Dutch imports from Asia, for example, including more than 50% of textiles and around 80% of silks.<sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> From Bengal, saltpeter was also shipped to Europe, opium was sold in Indonesia, raw silk was exported to Japan and the Netherlands, and cotton and silk textiles were exported to Europe, Indonesia and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
Akbar played a key role in establishing Bengal as a leading economic centre, as he began transforming many of the jungles there into farms. As soon as he conquered the region, he brought tools and men to clear jungles in order to expand cultivation, and brought <a href="/wiki/Sufis" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufis">Sufis</a> to open the jungles to farming.<sup id="cite_ref-Ludden96_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ludden96-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Bengal was later described as the <i>Paradise of Nations</i> by Mughal emperors.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The Mughals introduced <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_reform" title="Agrarian reform">agrarian reforms</a>, including the modern <a href="/wiki/Bengali_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali calendar">Bengali calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The calendar played a vital role in developing and organising harvests, tax collection and Bengali culture in general, including the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_New_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali New Year">New Year</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nabanna" title="Nabanna">Autumn</a> festivals. The province was a leading producer of grains, salt, fruits, liquors and wines, precious metals and ornaments.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2019)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Handloom" class="mw-redirect" title="Handloom">handloom</a> industry flourished under <a href="/wiki/Royal_warrant_of_appointment" title="Royal warrant of appointment">royal warrants</a>, making the region a hub of the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Muslin_trade_in_Bengal" title="Muslin trade in Bengal">muslin trade</a>, which peaked in the 17th and 18th centuries. The provincial capital <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a> became the commercial capital of the empire. The Mughals expanded cultivated land in the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_delta" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengal delta">Bengal delta</a> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufis</a>, which consolidated the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Bengali_Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali Muslim">Bengali Muslim</a> society.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2019)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>After 150 years of rule by Mughal <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">viceroys</a>, Bengal gained semi-independence as a <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominion</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Nawab_of_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Nawab of Bengal">Nawab of Bengal</a> in 1717. The Nawabs permitted European companies to set up trading posts across the region, including firms from <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_East_India_Company" title="French East India Company">France</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Bengal" title="Dutch Bengal">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish_India" title="Danish India">Denmark</a>, Portugal and <a href="/wiki/Bankipur_(Bengal)" title="Bankipur (Bengal)">Austria-Hungary</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Armenian_community_of_Dhaka" title="Armenian community of Dhaka">Armenian</a> community dominated banking and shipping in major cities and towns. The Europeans regarded Bengal as the richest place for trade.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> By the late 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> displaced the Mughal ruling class in Bengal.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_India#History" title="Demographics of India">Demographics of India: History</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h3>
<p>India's population growth accelerated under the Mughal Empire, with an unprecedented economic and demographic upsurge which boosted the Indian population by 60%<sup id="cite_ref-mcevedy184_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcevedy184-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> to 253% in 200 years during 1500–1700.<sup id="cite_ref-maddison236_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maddison236-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The Indian population had a faster growth during the Mughal era than at any known point in <a href="/wiki/Indian_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian history">Indian history</a> prior to the Mughal era.<sup id="cite_ref-mcevedy184_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcevedy184-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maddison2003_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddison2003-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The increased population growth rate was stimulated by Mughal <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_reform" title="Agrarian reform">agrarian reforms</a> that intensified agricultural production.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1995_9-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1995-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>190</span></sup> By the time of Aurangzeb's reign, there were a total of 455,698 villages in the Mughal Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-habib170_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-habib170-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The following table gives population estimates for the Mughal Empire, compared to the total population of India, including the regions of modern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, and compared to the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a>:
</p>
