<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urbanization">Urbanization</span></h3>
<p>Cities and towns boomed under the Mughal Empire, which had a relatively high degree of urbanization for its time, with 15% of its population living in urban centres.<sup id="cite_ref-Eraly2007_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2007-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> This was higher than the percentage of the urban population in contemporary Europe at the time and higher than that of <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a> in the 19th century;<sup id="cite_ref-Eraly2007_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2007-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> the level of urbanization in Europe did not reach 15% until the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Akbar's reign in 1600, the Mughal Empire's urban population was up to 17 million people, 15% of the empire's total population. This was larger than the entire urban population in Europe at the time, and even a century later in 1700, the urban population of England, Scotland and Wales did not exceed 13% of its total population,<sup id="cite_ref-habib170_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-habib170-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> while British India had an urban population that was under 13% of its total population in 1800 and 9% in 1881, a decline from the earlier Mughal era.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> By 1700, Mughal India had an urban population of 23 million people, larger than British India's urban population of 22.3 million in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Nizamuddin_Ahmad" title="Nizamuddin Ahmad">Nizamuddin Ahmad</a> (1551–1621) reported that, under Akbar's reign, there were 120 large cities and 3200 townships.<sup id="cite_ref-Eraly2007_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2007-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> A number of cities in India had a population between a quarter-million and half-million people,<sup id="cite_ref-Eraly2007_107-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly2007-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> with larger cities including <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Agra_Subah" title="Agra Subah">Agra Subah</a>) with up to 800,000 people, <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Lahore Subah">Lahore Subah</a>) with up to 700,000 people,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Subah" title="Bengal Subah">Bengal Subah</a>) with over 1 million people,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> and Delhi (in <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Subah" class="mw-redirect" title="Delhi Subah">Delhi Subah</a>) with over 600,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-Moosvi2008_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moosvi2008-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cities acted as markets for the sale of goods, and provided homes for a variety of merchants, traders, shopkeepers, artisans, moneylenders, weavers, craftspeople, officials, and religious figures.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt2015_64-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2015-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> However, a number of cities were military and political centres, rather than manufacturing or commerce centres.<sup id="cite_ref-Chaudhuri2008_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chaudhuri2008-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Persian_culture" title="Indo-Persian culture">Indo-Persian culture</a></div>
<p>The Mughal Empire was definitive in the early-modern and modern periods of South Asian history, with its legacy in India, Pakistan, Bangladesh and Afghanistan seen in cultural contributions such as:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taj_Mahal_(Edited).jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg/220px-Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg/330px-Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg/440px-Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3840" data-file-height="2525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taj_Mahal_(Edited).jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a> in Agra, India</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Centralized imperial rule that consolidated the smaller polities of South Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The amalgamation of <a href="/wiki/Persian_art" title="Persian art">Persian art</a> and literature with <a href="/wiki/Indian_art" title="Indian art">Indian art</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-r1_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r1-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Badshahi_Mosque,_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg/220px-Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg/330px-Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg/440px-Badshahi_Mosque%2C_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4900" data-file-height="3412" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Badshahi_Mosque,_Lahore._King%27s_Mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Badshahi Mosque, <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjab_(Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab (Pakistan)">Punjab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>The development of <a href="/wiki/Mughlai_cuisine" title="Mughlai cuisine">Mughlai cuisine</a>, an amalgamation of South Asian, Iranian and Central Asian culinary styles.</li>
<li>The development of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_clothing" title="Mughal clothing">Mughal clothing</a>, jewelry and fashion, utilizing richly decorated fabrics such as muslin, silk, brocade and velvet.</li>
<li>The standardization of the <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani language</a> (the colloquial language of <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a>), and thus the development of <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The introduction of sophisticated Iranian-style waterworks and horticulture through <a href="/wiki/Mughal_gardens" title="Mughal gardens">Mughal gardening</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Turkish_bath" title="Turkish bath">Turkish baths</a> into the Indian subcontinent.</li>
<li>The evolution and refinement of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian architecture">Indian architecture</a> and in turn, the development of later Rajput and Sikh palatial architecture. A famous Mughal landmark is the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>.</li>
<li>The development of the <a href="/wiki/Pehlwani" title="Pehlwani">Pehlwani</a> style of <a href="/wiki/Indian_wrestling" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian wrestling">Indian wrestling</a>, a combination of Indian <a href="/wiki/Malla-yuddha" title="Malla-yuddha">malla-yuddha</a> and Persian <a href="/wiki/Varzesh-e_bastani" class="mw-redirect" title="Varzesh-e bastani">varzesh-e bastani</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alter1992a_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alter1992a-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alter1992b_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alter1992b-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The construction of <a href="/wiki/Maktab" class="mw-redirect" title="Maktab">Maktab</a> schools, where youth were taught the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Fatawa-e-Alamgiri" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatawa-e-Alamgiri">Fatawa-i-Alamgiri</a></i> in their indigenous languages.</li>
<li>The development of <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_classical_music" title="Hindustani classical music">Hindustani classical music</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> and instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Sitar" title="Sitar">sitar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lata_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lata-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg/220px-Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg/330px-Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg/440px-Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2938" data-file-height="2536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fatehput_Sikiri_Buland_Darwaza_gate_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Buland_Darwaza" title="Buland Darwaza">Buland Darwaza</a> in Fatehpur Sikiri, Agra, India</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Islamic_architecture" title="Indo-Islamic architecture">Indo-Islamic architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal architecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_gardens" title="Mughal gardens">Mughal gardens</a></div><p>
The Mughals made a major contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> with the development of their unique Indo-Persian <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">architecture</a>. Many monuments were built during the Mughal era by the Muslim emperors, especially <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>—a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a> considered to be "the jewel of Muslim art in India and one of the universally admired masterpieces of the world's heritage", attracting 7-8 million unique visitors a year. The palaces, tombs, gardens and forts built by the dynasty stand today in <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aurangabad,_Maharashtra" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurangabad, Maharashtra">Aurangabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatehpur_Sikri" title="Fatehpur Sikri">Fatehpur Sikri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaipur" title="Jaipur">Jaipur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheikhupura" title="Sheikhupura">Sheikhupura</a>, and many other cities of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> such as:</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Verinag_(4).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Verinag_%284%29.JPG/220px-Verinag_%284%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Verinag_%284%29.JPG/330px-Verinag_%284%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Verinag_%284%29.JPG/440px-Verinag_%284%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Verinag_(4).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Verinag" title="Verinag">Verinag Gardens</a> in Srinagar, Kashmir</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shalimar_Gardens,_Kashmir.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Shalimar_Gardens%2C_Kashmir.jpg/220px-Shalimar_Gardens%2C_Kashmir.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Shalimar_Gardens%2C_Kashmir.jpg/330px-Shalimar_Gardens%2C_Kashmir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Shalimar_Gardens%2C_Kashmir.jpg/440px-Shalimar_Gardens%2C_Kashmir.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4898" data-file-height="3265" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shalimar_Gardens,_Kashmir.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Shalimar_Bagh,_Srinagar" title="Shalimar Bagh, Srinagar">Shalimar Bagh</a> in Srinagar, Kashmir, India</div></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_literature">Art and literature</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mansur-8.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mansur-8.png/220px-Mansur-8.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mansur-8.png/330px-Mansur-8.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mansur-8.png/440px-Mansur-8.png 2x" data-file-width="2284" data-file-height="3407" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mansur-8.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration by the 17th-century Mughal artist Ustad Mansur</div></div></div><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">Mughal painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_art" title="Indian art">Indian art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_clothing" title="Mughal clothing">Mughal clothing</a></div><p>
The <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">Mughal artistic tradition</a> was eclectic, borrowing from Iranian, Indian and even Renaissance European stylistic and thematic elements.<sup id="cite_ref-r3_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r3-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Mughal emperors often hosted Iranian bookbinders, illustrators, painters and calligraphers from the Safavid court due to the commonalities of their Timurid styles, and due to the Mughal affinity for Iranian art and calligraphy.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Persian miniatures commissioned during the Mughal period focus on eventful historical or political scenes such as Iranian and Indian myths/romances, major battles, court life and marriages, but also display a profound appreciation for the serenity and beauty of the natural world.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> For example, Emperor Jahangir commissioned brilliant artists such as <a href="/wiki/Ustad_Mansur" title="Ustad Mansur">Ustad Mansur</a> to realistically portray unusual flora and fauna throughout the empire. </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg/220px-Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg/330px-Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg/440px-Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1169" data-file-height="1798" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basawan_-_Alexander_Visits_the_Sage_Plato.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Alexander Visits the Sage Plato in His Mountain Cave" illustration by the 16th century Indian artist Basawan, in a folio from a quintet of the 13th century Indian poet Amir Khusrau Dihlavi</div></div></div><p>The literary works Jahangir ordered ranged from epics like the <i>Razmnama</i> (a Persian translation of the Hindu epic, the <i>Mahabharata</i>) to historical memoirs and recountings, including an illustrated version of the history of his reign -- <i>Tuzuk-i Jahangiri</i>. Richly-finished albums decorated with calligraphy and artistic scenes were mounted onto pages with decorative borders and then bound with covers of stamped and gilded or painted and lacquered leather.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Persian_language_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Persian language in the Indian subcontinent">Persian language in the Indian subcontinent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_and_Urdu" title="Persian and Urdu">Persian and Urdu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani language</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zaban_urdu_mualla.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Zaban_urdu_mualla.png/220px-Zaban_urdu_mualla.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Zaban_urdu_mualla.png 1.5x" data-file-width="225" data-file-height="184" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zaban_urdu_mualla.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The phrase <i lang="ur-Latn" title="Urdu-language romanization">Zuban-i Urdū-yi Muʿallá</i> ("Language of the exalted Horde") written in <a href="/wiki/Nasta%CA%BFl%C4%ABq_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Nastaʿlīq script">Nastaʿlīq script</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lashkari_Zaban_in_Nastaliq_script.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Lashkari_Zaban_in_Nastaliq_script.png/220px-Lashkari_Zaban_in_Nastaliq_script.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="85" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Lashkari_Zaban_in_Nastaliq_script.png/330px-Lashkari_Zaban_in_Nastaliq_script.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Lashkari_Zaban_in_Nastaliq_script.png 2x" data-file-width="407" data-file-height="157" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lashkari_Zaban_in_Nastaliq_script.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lashkari_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Lashkari language"><i>Lashkari Zabān</i></a> ("military camp language" or "Hordish language") title in Nastaʿlīq script</div></div></div>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> was the dominant and "official" language of the empire, the language of the elite was a Persianised form of <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a> called <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>. The language was written in a type of <a href="/wiki/Perso-Arabic_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Perso-Arabic script">Perso-Arabic script</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Nastaliq" class="mw-redirect" title="Nastaliq">Nastaliq</a>, and with literary conventions and specialised vocabulary borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a>; the dialect was eventually given its own name of Urdu.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Mughals spoke Urdu,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> and by the year 1700, the Mughals had formalized the language.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Modern <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>, which uses <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>-based vocabulary along with Perso-Arabic loanwords, is <a href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a> with Urdu.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Mughal_Empire" title="Army of the Mughal Empire">Army of the Mughal Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_weapons" title="Mughal weapons">Mughal weapons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_artillery" title="Mughal artillery">Mughal artillery</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gunpowder_warfare">Gunpowder warfare</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_empires" title="Gunpowder empires">Gunpowder empires</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_gunpowder#India_and_the_Mughal_Empire" title="History of gunpowder">History of gunpowder: India and the Mughal Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army,_c.1585_(colour_litho).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg/220px-Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg/330px-Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army%2C_c.1585_%28colour_litho%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="343" data-file-height="428" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Officer_of_the_Mughal_Army,_c.1585_(colour_litho).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mughal <a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">matchlock</a> rifle, 16th century.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mughal_musket.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mughal_musket.png/220px-Mughal_musket.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mughal_musket.png/330px-Mughal_musket.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mughal_musket.png/440px-Mughal_musket.png 2x" data-file-width="492" data-file-height="468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mughal_musket.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mughal <a href="/wiki/Musketeer" title="Musketeer">musketeer</a>, 16th century.</div></div></div>
<p>Mughal India was one of the three Islamic <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_empires" title="Gunpowder empires">gunpowder empires</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Safavid_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavid Persia">Safavid Persia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hodgson_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgson-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Streusand_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Streusand-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> By the time he was invited by <a href="/wiki/Lodi_dynasty" title="Lodi dynasty">Lodi</a> governor of <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daulat_Khan_Lodi" title="Daulat Khan Lodi">Daulat Khan</a>, to support his rebellion against Lodi <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Lodi" title="Ibrahim Lodi">Ibrahim Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> was familiar with <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Field_artillery" title="Field artillery">field artillery</a>, and a method for deploying them.  Babur had employed Ottoman expert <a href="/wiki/Ustad_Ali_Quli" title="Ustad Ali Quli">Ustad Ali Quli</a>, who showed Babur the standard Ottoman formation—artillery and firearm-equipped infantry protected by wagons in the center and the <a href="/wiki/Mounted_archer" class="mw-redirect" title="Mounted archer">mounted archers</a> on both wings. Babur used this formation at the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Panipat" title="First Battle of Panipat">First Battle of Panipat</a> in 1526, where the <a href="/wiki/Afghan" title="Afghan">Afghan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> forces loyal to the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>, though superior in numbers but without the gunpowder weapons, were defeated. The decisive victory of the Timurid forces is one reason opponents rarely met Mughal princes in pitched battle over the course of the empire's history.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> In India, guns made of <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> were recovered from <a href="/wiki/Kozhikode" title="Kozhikode">Calicut</a> (1504) and <a href="/wiki/Diu,_India" title="Diu, India">Diu</a> (1533).<sup id="cite_ref-Partington226_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Partington226-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fathullah_Shirazi" title="Fathullah Shirazi">Fathullah Shirazi</a> (c. 1582), a Persian polymath and mechanical engineer who worked for Akbar, developed an early multi gun shot. As opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Polybolos" title="Polybolos">polybolos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Repeating_crossbow" title="Repeating crossbow">repeating crossbows</a> used earlier in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> and China, respectively, Shirazi's rapid-firing gun had multiple <a href="/wiki/Gun_barrel" title="Gun barrel">gun barrels</a> that fired <a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">hand cannons</a> loaded with gunpowder. It may be considered a version of a <a href="/wiki/Volley_gun" title="Volley gun">volley gun</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fathullah_Shirazi_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fathullah_Shirazi-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 17th century, Indians were manufacturing a diverse variety of firearms; large guns in particular, became visible in <a href="/wiki/Tanjore" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanjore">Tanjore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dacca" class="mw-redirect" title="Dacca">Dacca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bijapur,_Karnataka" class="mw-redirect" title="Bijapur, Karnataka">Bijapur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Murshidabad" title="Murshidabad">Murshidabad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gujar%C4%81t" class="mw-redirect" title="Gujarāt">Gujarāt</a> supplied Europe <a href="/wiki/Saltpeter" class="mw-redirect" title="Saltpeter">saltpeter</a> for use in <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Gunpowder warfare">gunpowder warfare</a> during the 17th century,<sup id="cite_ref-IndiaBritannica_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndiaBritannica-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal Bengal">Mughal Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malwa" title="Malwa">Mālwa</a> also participated in saltpeter production.<sup id="cite_ref-IndiaBritannica_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndiaBritannica-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The Dutch, French, Portuguese and English used <a href="/wiki/Chhapra" title="Chhapra">Chāpra</a> as a center of saltpeter refining.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rocketry_and_explosives">Rocketry and explosives</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mysorean_rockets" title="Mysorean rockets">Mysorean rockets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congreve_rocket" title="Congreve rocket">Congreve rocket</a></div>
<p>In the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> was the first to initiate and use metal cylinder <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rockets</a> known as <i>bans</i>, particularly against <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephants</a>, during the Battle of Sanbal.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> In 1657, the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal Army">Mughal Army</a> used rockets during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Bidar" title="Siege of Bidar">Siege of Bidar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yazdani15_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yazdani15-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Prince Aurangzeb's forces discharged rockets and <a href="/wiki/Grenade" title="Grenade">grenades</a> while scaling the walls. Sidi Marjan was mortally wounded when a rocket struck his large gunpowder depot, and after twenty-seven days of hard fighting Bidar was captured by the victorious Mughals.<sup id="cite_ref-Yazdani15_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yazdani15-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>A History of Greek Fire and Gunpowder</i>, <a href="/wiki/James_Riddick_Partington" class="mw-redirect" title="James Riddick Partington">James Riddick Partington</a> described Indian rockets and <a href="/wiki/Explosive_weapon" title="Explosive weapon">explosive</a> <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">mines</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Partington226_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Partington226-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>The Indian war rockets were formidable weapons before such rockets were used in Europe. They had bam-boo rods, a rocket-body lashed to the rod, and iron points. They were directed at the target and fired by lighting the fuse, but the trajectory was rather erratic. The use of mines and counter-mines with explosive charges of gunpowder is mentioned for the times of Akbar and <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahāngir</a>.</p></blockquote>
<p>Later, the <a href="/wiki/Mysorean_rockets" title="Mysorean rockets">Mysorean rockets</a> were upgraded versions of Mughal rockets used during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jinji" title="Siege of Jinji">Siege of Jinji</a> by the progeny of the <a href="/wiki/Nawab_of_Arcot" class="mw-redirect" title="Nawab of Arcot">Nawab of Arcot</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hyder_Ali" title="Hyder Ali">Hyder Ali</a>'s father <a href="/wiki/Fatah_Muhammad" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatah Muhammad">Fatah Muhammad</a> the constable at <a href="/wiki/Budikote" title="Budikote">Budikote</a>, commanded a corps consisting of 50 rocketmen (<i>Cushoon</i>) for the Nawab of Arcot. Hyder Ali realised the importance of rockets and introduced advanced versions of metal cylinder rockets. These rockets turned fortunes in favour of the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Mysore" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Mysore">Sultanate of Mysore</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Mysore_War" title="Second Anglo-Mysore War">Second Anglo-Mysore War</a>, particularly during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pollilur" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Pollilur">Battle of Pollilur</a>. In turn, the Mysorean rockets were the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Congreve_rocket" title="Congreve rocket">Congreve rockets</a>, which Britain deployed in the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> against France and the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> against the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world">Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_astronomy" title="Indian astronomy">Indian astronomy</a></div>
<p>While there appears to have been little concern for <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_astronomy" title="Theoretical astronomy">theoretical astronomy</a>, Mughal  <a href="/wiki/Astronomers" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomers">astronomers</a>  made advances in <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">observational astronomy</a> and produced nearly a hundred <i><a href="/wiki/Zij" title="Zij">Zij</a></i> treatises. <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a> built a personal <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatory</a> near Delhi; Jahangir and Shah Jahan were also intending to build observatories, but were unable to do so. The <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_instruments" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomical instruments">astronomical instruments</a> and observational techniques used at the Mughal observatories were mainly derived from <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world">Islamic astronomy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sharma_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baber_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baber-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> In the 17th century, the Mughal Empire saw a synthesis between Islamic and <a href="/wiki/Hindu_astronomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu astronomy">Hindu astronomy</a>, where Islamic observational instruments were combined with <a href="/wiki/Indian_mathematics" title="Indian mathematics">Hindu computational</a> techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-Sharma_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baber_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baber-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the decline of the Mughal Empire, the Hindu king <a href="/wiki/Jai_Singh_II_of_Amber" class="mw-redirect" title="Jai Singh II of Amber">Jai Singh II of Amber</a> continued the work of Mughal <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>. In the early 18th century, he built several large observatories called <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yantra_Mandirs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Yantra Mandirs (page does not exist)">Yantra Mandirs</a>, in order to rival <a href="/wiki/Ulugh_Beg" title="Ulugh Beg">Ulugh Beg</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> <a href="/wiki/Ulugh_Beg_Observatory" title="Ulugh Beg Observatory">observatory</a>, and in order to improve on the earlier Hindu computations in the <i> <a href="/wiki/Siddhantas" class="mw-redirect" title="Siddhantas">Siddhantas</a></i> and Islamic observations in <i><a href="/wiki/Zij-i-Sultani" class="mw-redirect" title="Zij-i-Sultani">Zij-i-Sultani</a></i>. The instruments he used were influenced by Islamic astronomy, while the computational techniques were derived from Hindu astronomy.<sup id="cite_ref-Sharma_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baber_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baber-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_medieval_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam">Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sake_Dean_Mahomed" class="mw-redirect" title="Sake Dean Mahomed">Sake Dean Mahomed</a> had learned much of Mughal <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> and understood the techniques used to produce various <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a> and soaps to produce <a href="/wiki/Shampoo" title="Shampoo">shampoo</a>. He was also a notable writer who described the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Shah_Alam_II" title="Shah Alam II">Shah Alam II</a> and the cities of <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a> and Delhi in rich detail and also made note of the glories of the Mughal Empire.
</p><p>In Britain, Sake Dean Mahomed was appointed as <a href="/wiki/Shampoo_(massage)" title="Shampoo (massage)">shampooing surgeon</a> to both Kings <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Teltscher_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teltscher-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgy">Metallurgy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_metallurgy_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="History of metallurgy in the Indian subcontinent">History of metallurgy in the Indian subcontinent</a></div>
<p>One of the most remarkable astronomical instruments invented in Mughal India is the seamless <a href="/wiki/Celestial_globe" title="Celestial globe">celestial globe</a>. It was invented in <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> by Ali Kashmiri ibn Luqman in 998 <a href="/wiki/Anno_Hegirae" class="mw-redirect" title="Anno Hegirae">AH</a> (1589–90 CE), and twenty other such <a href="/wiki/Globe" title="Globe">globes</a> were later produced in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> and Kashmir during the Mughal Empire. Before they were rediscovered in the 1980s, it was believed by modern <a href="/wiki/Metallurgists" class="mw-redirect" title="Metallurgists">metallurgists</a> to be technically impossible to produce metal globes without any <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/seam" class="extiw" title="wikt:seam">seams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The title (Mirza) descends to all the sons of the family, without exception. In the royal family it is placed after the name instead of before it, thus, Abbas Mirza and Hosfiein Mirza. Mirza is a civil title, and Khan is a military one. The title of Khan is creative, but not hereditary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorier1812601_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorier1812601-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-Richards1995-2-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Richards1995-2_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFRichards1995" class="citation">Richards, John F. (1995), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=HHyVh29gy4QC&amp;pg=PA2"><i>The Mughal Empire</i></a>, Cambridge University Press, p.&#160;2, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-56603-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-56603-2"><bdi>978-0-521-56603-2</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Mughal+Empire&amp;rft.pages=2&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1995&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-56603-2&amp;rft.aulast=Richards&amp;rft.aufirst=John+F.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DHHyVh29gy4QC%26pg%3DPA2&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMughal+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "Although the first two Timurid emperors and many of their noblemen were recent migrants to the subcontinent, the dynasty and the empire itself became indisputably Indian. The interests and futures of all concerned were in India, not in ancestral homelands in the Middle East or Central Asia. Furthermore, the Mughal Empire emerged from the Indian historical experience. It was the end product of a millennium of Muslim conquest, colonization, and state-building in the Indian subcontinent."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Stein2010-12-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Stein2010-12_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFStein2010" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Burton_Stein" title="Burton Stein">Stein, Burton</a> (2010), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=QY4zdTDwMAQC&amp;pg=PA159"><i>A History of India</i></a>, John Wiley &amp; Sons, pp.&#160;159–, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4443-2351-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4443-2351-1"><bdi>978-1-4443-2351-1</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+India&amp;rft.pages=159-&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4443-2351-1&amp;rft.aulast=Stein&amp;rft.aufirst=Burton&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DQY4zdTDwMAQC%26pg%3DPA159&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMughal+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "The realm so defined and governed was a vast territory of some 750,000 square miles, ranging from the frontier with Central Asia in northern Afghanistan to the northern uplands of the Deccan plateau, and from the Indus basin on the west to the Assamese highlands in the east."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Peter_2006-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Peter_2006_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Turchin, Peter; Adams, Jonathan M.; Hall, Thomas D (December 2006). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jwsr.pitt.edu/ojs/index.php/jwsr/article/view/369/381">"East-West Orientation of Historical Empires"</a>. <i>Journal of World-Systems Research</i>. <b>12</b> (2): 223. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1076-156X">1076-156X</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-Gilbert2017-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gilbert2017_13-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFGilbert2017" class="citation">Gilbert, Marc Jason (2017), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=1dhKDgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA75"><i>South Asia in World History</i></a>, Oxford University Press, pp.&#160;75–, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-066137-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-066137-3"><bdi>978-0-19-066137-3</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=South+Asia+in+World+History&amp;rft.pages=75-&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2017&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-066137-3&amp;rft.aulast=Gilbert&amp;rft.aufirst=Marc+Jason&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D1dhKDgAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA75&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMughal+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "Babur then adroitly gave the Ottomans his promise not to attack them in return for their military aid, which he received in the form of the newest of battlefield inventions, the matchlock gun and cast cannons, as well as instructors to train his men to use them."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Stein2010-0-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Stein2010-0_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFStein2010" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Burton_Stein" title="Burton Stein">Stein, Burton</a> (2010), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=QY4zdTDwMAQC&amp;pg=PA159"><i>A History of India</i></a>, John Wiley &amp; Sons, pp.&#160;159–, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4443-2351-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4443-2351-1"><bdi>978-1-4443-2351-1</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+India&amp;rft.pages=159-&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4443-2351-1&amp;rft.aulast=Stein&amp;rft.aufirst=Burton&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DQY4zdTDwMAQC%26pg%3DPA159&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMughal+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "Another possible date for the beginning of the Mughal regime is 1600, when the institutions that defined the regime were set firmly in place and when the heartland of the empire was defined; both of these were the accomplishment of Babur’s grandson Akbar."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Stein2010-1-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Stein2010-1_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFStein2010" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Burton_Stein" title="Burton Stein">Stein, Burton</a> (2010), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=QY4zdTDwMAQC&amp;pg=PA159"><i>A History of India</i></a>, John Wiley &amp; Sons, pp.&#160;159–, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4443-2351-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4443-2351-1"><bdi>978-1-4443-2351-1</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+India&amp;rft.pages=159-&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4443-2351-1&amp;rft.aulast=Stein&amp;rft.aufirst=Burton&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DQY4zdTDwMAQC%26pg%3DPA159&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMughal+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "The imperial career of the Mughal house is conventionally reckoned to have ended in 1707 when the emperor Aurangzeb, a fifth-generation descendant of Babur, died. His fifty-year reign began in 1658 with the Mughal state seeming as strong as ever or even stronger. But in Aurangzeb’s later years the state was brought to the brink of destruction, over which it toppled within a decade and a half after his death; by 1720 imperial Mughal rule was largely finished and an epoch of two imperial centuries had closed."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Richards1995-3-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Richards1995-3_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFRichards1995" class="citation">Richards, John F. </li>
<li id="cite_note-Stein2010-2-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Stein2010-2_17-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFStein2010" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Burton_Stein" title="Burton Stein">Stein, Burton</a> (2010), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=QY4zdTDwMAQC&amp;pg=PA159"><i>A History of India</i></a>, John Wiley &amp; Sons, pp.&#160;159–, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4443-2351-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4443-2351-1"><bdi>978-1-4443-2351-1</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+India&amp;rft.pages=159-&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4443-2351-1&amp;rft.aulast=Stein&amp;rft.aufirst=Burton&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DQY4zdTDwMAQC%26pg%3DPA159&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMughal+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "The vaunting of such progenitors pointed up the central character of the Mughal regime as a warrior state: it was born in war and it was sustained by war until the eighteenth century, when warfare destroyed it."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Robb2011-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Robb2011_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFRobb2011" class="citation">Robb, Peter (2011), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=GQ-2VH1LO_EC&amp;pg=PA108"><i>A History of India</i></a>, Macmillan, pp.&#160;108–, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-230-34549-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-230-34549-2"><bdi>978-0-230-34549-2</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+India&amp;rft.pages=108-&amp;rft.pub=Macmillan&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-230-34549-2&amp;rft.aulast=Robb&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DGQ-2VH1LO_EC%26pg%3DPA108&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMughal+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "The Mughal state was geared for war, and succeeded while it won its battles.  It controlled territory partly through its network of strongholds, from its fortified capitals in Agra, Delhi or Lahore, which defined its heartlands, to the converted and expanded forts of Rajasthan and the Deccan. The emperors' will was frequently enforced in battle. Hundreds of army scouts were an important source of information. But the empire's administrative structure too was defined by and directed at war. Local military checkpoints or thanas kept order. Directly appointed imperial military and civil commanders (faujdars) controlled the cavalry and infantry, or the administration, in each region. The peasantry in turn were often armed, able to provide supporters for regional powers, and liable to rebellion on their own account: continual pacification was required of the rulers."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Gilbert2017-2-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gilbert2017-2_19-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFGilbert2017" class="citation">Gilbert, Marc Jason (2017), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=1dhKDgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA75"><i>South Asia in World History</i></a>, Oxford University Press, pp.&#160;75–, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-066137-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-066137-3"><bdi>978-0-19-066137-3</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=South+Asia+in+World+History&amp;rft.pages=75-&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2017&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-066137-3&amp;rft.aulast=Gilbert&amp;rft.aufirst=Marc+Jason&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D1dhKDgAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA75&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMughal+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "With Safavid and Ottoman aid, the Mughals would soon join these two powers in a triumvirate of warrior-driven, expansionist, and both militarily and bureaucratically efficient early modern states, now often called "gunpowder empires" due to their common proficiency is using such weapons to conquer lands they sought to control."</span>
</li>
